Montgomery County v. Walsh


No. 201, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


274 Md. 502; 336 A.2d 97; 1975 Md. LEXIS 1224



April 14, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Notice of Appeal to Supreme Court of the United States Filed June 2, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Moorman, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed; respondents to pay costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, county and officials, sought review of an order of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which granted plaintiff county employees and spouses' motion for summary judgment, declaring a particular county ordinance unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, county employees and spouses, filed a bill of complaint for declaratory judgment and injunctive relief against defendants, county and officials, alleging that the ordinance, which required them to file the financial disclosure statements was unconstitutional and void on its face because it conflicted with, or was violative of, numerous provisions of the federal and state constitutions and other laws of the state. The circuit court declared the ordinance unconstitutional and null and void in its entirety and granted plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court reversed the award of summary judgment, ruling that the circuit court erred in finding that the ordinance was void. The court held that the county ordinance facially demonstrated a compelling interest, which was subordinated to any resulting infringement on plaintiffs' right to privacy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the award of summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs, county employees and spouses, ruling that the county ordinance facially demonstrated a compelling interest, which was subordinated to any resulting infringement on the right of privacy.

 

471 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Carter v. State


No. 51, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


274 Md. 411; 337 A.2d 415; 1975 Md. LEXIS 1220



April 11, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland. Circuit Court for Prince George's County, Couch, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Special Appeals reversed; case remanded to that Court with directions to reverse the judgment of the Circuit Court for Prince George's County and remand the case for a new trial; costs to be paid by Prince George's County, Maryland.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Special Appeals of Maryland affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of heroin with intent to distribute in violation of Md. Code art. 27, § 286(a)(1), finding that ample probable cause was established in the affidavit for a search warrant. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to a search and seizure warrant that was based on a detective's affidavit, the police seized over 100 grams of heroin from defendant's residence. After his motion to suppress the evidence was denied, defendant was convicted of possessing heroin with intent to distribute. The lower court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court reversed and remanded. It held that if any conversation of defendant or any conversation overheard upon his premises, whether he was present and participating in it or not, was subjected to a "search and seizure" by the use of any wiretap or eavesdropping device in violation of his rights under the Fourth Amendment, any information garnered as "fruits" of such primary illegality and "come upon" by the "exploitation" of that illegality could not be used as derivative evidence for an application for a search and seizure warrant. To hold otherwise would have permitted the prosecution to use knowledge acquired in violation of the Fourth Amendment and gain by its own wrong. Thus, defendant was entitled to an evidentiary hearing on the motion to suppress in order to determine whether or not the facts to which the affidavit was made were tainted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the lower court's judgment with directions to reverse the trial court's judgment and remand the case for a new trial.

 

472 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Bringe v. Collins


No. 107, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


274 Md. 338; 335 A.2d 670; 1975 Md. LEXIS 1215



April 9, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County; Couch, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed. Petitioner to pay costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner tenant sought review of a judgment from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland), which denied his request for a declaratory judgment that Md. Code Ann., Real Prop. § 8-402(b) (1974), was in violation of both U.S. Const. amend. VII and Md. Const. art. XV, § 6, because it failed to provide for a jury trial in an action by a landlord to recover possession of property. Petitioner also sought an injunction.


OVERVIEW: After respondent landlord was granted possession of the premises from petitioner tenant, petitioner brought a declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of Md. Code Ann., Real Prop. § 8-402(b) (1974), the statutory basis of the possession action. The issue was whether there was a right to a jury trial in an action by a landlord to recover possession from a tenant holding over after the expiration of the lease. Petitioner's request for declaratory judgment was denied, and the court affirmed on appeal. In the possession action, neither party sought money damages or requested a jury. The Seventh Amendment to the U.S. Constitution provided that in suits at common law, where the value in controversy exceeded 20 dollars, the right of trial by jury was preserved, and no fact tried by a jury should be otherwise reexamined in any court of the United States, than according to the rules of the common law. However, the Supreme Court of the United States had consistently held that the Seventh Amendment was not incorporated into the Fourteenth Amendment, and consequently was not applicable to state court proceedings. In addition, petitioner waived any right to a jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment denying petitioner tenant's request for a declaratory judgment that a statute was in violation of state and federal constitutions because it failed to provide for a jury trial in an action by a landlord to recover possession of property. The court held that there was no right to a jury trial in an action by a landlord to recover possession of property.

 

473 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Bowie Inn, Inc. v. Bowie


No. 58, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


274 Md. 230; 335 A.2d 679; 1975 Md. LEXIS 1208; 7 ERC (BNA) 2083; 73 A.L.R.3d 1079



March 24, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Two appeals in one record from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County; McCullough, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed. Petitioners to pay costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, retail merchants and soft drink association (retailers), challenged the judgment by the Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland) that was entered in favor of defendant city in the retailers' actions that alleged a city ordinance that made it unlawful to sell any soft drink or malt beverage unless a deposit of at least five cents on each container was charged at retail level and then given back was unlawful.


OVERVIEW: The retailers contended the ordinance violated due process as it imposed a mode of doing business which bore no substantial relation to the reduction of liter, was vague, and denied the retailers equal protection of the laws and violated the Commerce Clause. The retailers also claimed that the city was not authorized to enact the ordinance and that the state legislature had preempted the matter of deposits requirements. The court disagreed and affirmed, finding that the retailers failed to prove the ordinance bore no real and substantial relation to the public health, morals, safety, and welfare of the citizens of the city; thus, the ordinance was not arbitrary, and there was no denial of due process. The court held the definition of soft drink was not vague as it gave a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice, and therefore, the ordinance, a criminal statute, was not void for vagueness. The court found the ordinance did not create an artificial and arbitrary classification of soft drink and malt beverage containers, the ordinance did not place an unconstitutional burden on interstate commerce, and the city had the power to have enact the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that held the city ordinance was constitutional.

 

474 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Department of Natural Resources v. Linchester Sand & Gravel Corp.


No. 80, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


274 Md. 211; 334 A.2d 514; 1975 Md. LEXIS 1207



March 19, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Somerset County; Duer, C. J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Circuit Court for Somerset County vacated. Case remanded for further proceedings to be conducted in accordance with this opinion. Costs to be paid by the appellee.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee landowner, without a permit, created a cut into his private wetland that resulted in the despoliation of a considerable portion of both his and the adjoining public marsh. When appellant Department of Natural Resources (department) denied him a permit to continue his building project he appealed the decision to the Circuit Court for Somerset County (Maryland). The department challenged a judgment in favor of the landowner.


OVERVIEW: The department argued that the extensive and nullifying judicial de novo review, allowed under the wetlands statute, Md. Code Ann., Nat. Res. § 9-308(b), was unconstitutional because it allowed the judicial branch to usurp the province of the administrative agency. The court held that the decisions of the department whether the land came within the statutory wetlands designation and whether it had to remain in its then existing natural state were decisions made pursuant to the state's police power within the guidelines and standards prescribed by the legislature. On appeal, a circuit court was constitutionally limited to an assessment of whether that determination was based on evidence sufficiently substantial so that the permit denial was not arbitrary and capricious. Md. Code Ann., Nat. Res. § 9-308(b), which provided for a de novo trial free of the Administrative Procedure Act, with the right of electing to have a jury trial and with no right of removal, was void as unconstitutional. The circuit court was not allowed to empanel a jury and instruct it to convert itself into an administrative body with the authority to grant or deny the permit and to apply the wetlands statute.


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of the landowner was vacated and the case was remanded for a limited review of whether the department's decision was arbitrary and capricious.

Board of County Comm'rs v. Colgan


No. 143, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


274 Md. 193; 334 A.2d 89; 1975 Md. LEXIS 1206



March 11, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland. Circuit Court for Prince George's County, Powers, C. J.



DISPOSITION: Order of Court of Special Appeals reversing order of Circuit Court for Prince George's County and ordering remand of case to Workmen's Compensation Commission affirmed; costs to be paid by petitioner.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Board of County Commissioners sought review of an order from the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland, which reversed the trial court's order that denied respondent firefighter benefits and ruled that the Workmen's Compensation Act (Act), Md. Ann. Code art. 101, § 64(A) (1957; Cum. Supp. 1971), was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The firefighter filed with the commission a claim for compensation as a consequence of a heart attack he sustained. The commission denied his claim. On appeal, the trial court affirmed the commission's order, but held that the Act was unconstitutional. The trial court's finding was based on the variance between the title of the Act, which referred to a presumption of compensable occupational disease, and the body of the Act which, without referring to "occupational disease" made injuries caused by various diseases presumptively "compensable." On review, the court of special appeals reversed the trial court's order for consideration as for an occupational disease. On certiorari, the court affirmed the court of special appeals. The court held that there was no repugnancy between the title and body of the act. The body of the act provided that certain conditions were compensable under art 101. The title of the Act in no way conflicted with that purpose. The title simply made clear that the Act should have been interpreted as granting such benefits in accordance with the provisions of that article as they related to occupational diseases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the court of special appeals that reversed the trial court's order that denied the firefighter benefits and ruled the Act unconstitutional, and remanded the case to the commission.

Criminal Injuries Compensation Bd. v. Gould


No. 2, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


273 Md. 486; 331 A.2d 55; 1975 Md. LEXIS 1369



January 16, 1975, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Cahoon, J.



DISPOSITION: Order of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County affirmed; costs to be paid by the appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Criminal Injuries Compensation Board sought review from a Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) judgment vacating its decision to deny appellee cabdriver relief under the Criminal Injuries Compensation Act, Md. Ann. Code art. 26A, §1 et seq., arguing that no judicial review of its decision had been permitted.


OVERVIEW: A cabdriver sought compensation from the Criminal Injuries Compensation Board under the Criminal Injuries Compensation Act, Md. Ann. Code art. 26A, §1 et seq., for injuries suffered when he was shot in the neck and back of his head during a robbery conducted while he was operating his taxi. After the Board disallowed the claim, the cabdriver sought judicial review and the Board's decision was vacated. The Board appealed, arguing that under Md. Ann. Code art. 26A, §10(a) the decision had not been subject to judicial review. The court held that (1) the status of the cabdriver as an independent contractor rather than as an employee had not been disputed and had in fact been supported by evidence including statements from the cab company from which he rented his taxi, (2) Md. Ann. Code art. 26A, §10(a) did not prohibit judicial review of improper exercises of administrative powers, (3) the petition for review had asserted arbitrary, illegal, capricious, and unreasonable action by the Board, making the matter ripe for judicial review, and (4) a slight error in captioning the application for review did not prevent review.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment.

Public Service Com. v. Baltimore Gas & Electric Co.


No. 152, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


273 Md. 357; 329 A.2d 691; 1974 Md. LEXIS 712



December 19, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Four appeals in one record from the Circuit Court for Calvert County; Bowen, J.



DISPOSITION: Decree of Circuit Court for Calvert County vacated; order No. 60684 of the Public Service Commission in case No. 6700 affirmed as to all issues decided by this opinion; costs to be paid by appellee.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the Public Service Commission of Maryland, in a public service proceeding against defendant electric company, suspended the company's proposed rates for the maximum period authorized. The commission and the company sought review of the decree of the Circuit Court for Calvert County (Maryland), which reversed the commission's decision to use an average rate base in valuing the company's property for rate making purposes.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court vacated the decree of the circuit court and affirmed the original findings of the commission in its public service case. The court noted that unless shown to be unconstitutional, or without statutory authority or made upon unlawful procedure, or arbitrary or capricious, or unsupported by substantial evidence on the record, every final decision of the commission was prima facie correct. The court found that the commission's determination to employ an average rate base in valuing the company's property used and useful in rendering service to the public was not illegal, arbitrary, or capricious, but was supported by substantial evidence on the record. The court found that to the extent that the commission was reasonably convinced of the existence of problems of inflation, attrition, and regulatory lag, it could have attempted to meet them in various ways. The court found that the commission's decision regarding the rate of return and decision to continue to allow a composite depreciation rate and to deny an increase was not illegal, arbitrary, or capricious, and was supported by substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the determination of the circuit court and affirmed the prior decision of the commission in its public service case.

Citizens Planning & Housing Asso. v. County Executive of Baltimore County


No. 55, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


273 Md. 333; 329 A.2d 681; 1974 Md. LEXIS 710



December 18, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motion for Rehearing Filed January 16, 1975; Denied January 29, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland. Circuit Court for Baltimore County, Jenifer, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Special Appeals affirmed in part, reversed in part; remanded to that court with instructions to affirm in part, and reverse in part, the order of the Circuit Court for Baltimore County and to remand the case for further proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion; appellants to pay one-third of costs, appellees to pay two-thirds.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant organization and other challenged a decision of the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland affirming the judgment of the trial court sustaining appellee county's demurrer to organization and others' complaint for lack of standing.


OVERVIEW: Organizations and individuals filed an action against county on their own behalf and on behalf of all other county residents similarly situated. They alleged damages as county taxpayers and as real property owners. In sustaining county's demurrer, the circuit court held that organizations and others failed to allege any facts showing that county's actions would result in increased taxes or pecuniary loss to them. The court granted certiorari to consider the question of standing. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court held that the allegations of standing on the part of individuals were sufficient to withstand a demurrer, and the better-reasoned view supported them on the question of standing. However, the court found that the rule was that an organization lacked standing to sue where it has no property interest of its own, separate and distinct from that of its individual members, that was affected by any of the alleged acts. The court found that the bill of complaint included no allegations remotely suggesting that organizations possessed such separate interests, thus they lacked the necessary standing to maintain the action.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded.

Maryland State Bar Asso. v. Rosenberg


Misc. Docket (Subtitle BV) No. 6, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


273 Md. 351; 329 A.2d 106; 1974 Md. LEXIS 711



December 18, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions by the Maryland State Bar Association, Inc., and the Bar Association of Montgomery County, Maryland, for disciplinary action against Harvey Rosenberg, an attorney authorized to practice law in Maryland. The judicial panel (MATHIAS, Joseph M., and SHEARIN and CAHOON, JJ.) to which the charges were referred for hearing recommended disbarment and the respondent filed exceptions.



DISPOSITION: It is so ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a state bar association and a county bar association, filed separate petitions with the court charging respondent attorney with the commission of an act of professional misconduct, deceit, a crime involving moral turpitude, and conduct prejudicial to the administration of justice. A three-judge panel of the court recommended the attorney's disbarment. However, the attorney filed exceptions to the panel's recommendation.


OVERVIEW: The bar associations alleged that the attorney's misconduct constituted a violation of Md. Code Prof. Resp. DR 1-102(A)(1)-(6), Canon 1, and that the attorney was convicted by a federal court of violating 18 U.S.C.S. § 1621, regarding perjury before a federal grand jury. On the other hand, the attorney argued that his conviction was not a "final judgment" within the meaning of former Md. R. BV4(f)(1) because of a pending motion for a new trial based on newly discovered evidence and because the failure to hold a hearing on the "ultimate fact," regarding whether perjury was indeed committed, rendered the hearing held meaningless. On consideration of the panel's recommendation, the court agreed to disbar the attorney. Thus, the court ordered the attorney's name stricken from the rolls of those authorized to practice law in Maryland. In reaching its conclusion, the court noted that the attorney's motion for a new trial had been withdrawn in open court with prejudice and that the attorney had been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude. Therefore, the attorney's conduct plainly violated the disciplinary rules.


OUTCOME: The court adopted the panel's recommendation and disbarred the attorney.

Doe v. Commander, Wheaton Police Dep't


No. 63, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


273 Md. 262; 329 A.2d 35; 1974 Md. LEXIS 704



December 4, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Mitchell, John J., J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Order vacated; case remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion; costs to abide the result.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant arrestee sought review of the decision of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which granted defendant police department, officials, and county's motion for a demurrer in the arrestee's action that sought expungement of his criminal arrest records.


OVERVIEW: On appeal the arrestee argued that the trial court was empowered to order expungement or limit the dissemination of his criminal arrest record because there was no evidence of any kind that the arrestee committed any criminal offense. The arrestee had been arrested and later charged with committing an unnatural and perverted sexual act, but the charges were completely dismissed. The trial court granted the demurrer because it determined that it did not have subject matter jurisdiction to order the expungement of the records. The court vacated the order and held that the trial court mistakenly determined that it did not have subject matter jurisdiction. The court determined that the trial court, as an equity court, had the power to adjudicate the merits of the arrestee's petition, or grant any injunctive or other equitable relief requested, even absent specific legislative authorization.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order that granted the demurrer and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

 

481 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Henry v. State


No. 42, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


273 Md. 131; 328 A.2d 293; 1974 Md. LEXIS 695



November 25, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland. Criminal Court of Baltimore, Sodaro, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Special Appeals affirmed in part and vacated in part and case remanded to the Court of Special Appeals for the entry of an order affirming the judgment in the Criminal Court of Baltimore under Indictment No. 17203157 (receiving stolen goods) and of the judgment under Indictment No. 17203163 (larceny of automobile) other than as to sentence and as to that for further remand to the Criminal Court of Baltimore for the imposition of a valid sentence.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland, which affirmed his conviction for larceny of an automobile and for receiving stolen goods.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was complicit in the taking of an automobile and, when driving around later, was involved in a robbery and murder whereby he was outside of a store in the car while the perpetrators did the killing inside the store. Defendant gave the murderers a ride to and from the crime scene. Defendant was charged with the murder, as well as larceny of the automobile and receiving stolen goods, due to his getting money from the heist. The jury returned guilty verdicts only for larceny of the automobile and receiving stolen goods. The trial court sentenced defendant to the maximum it thought was applicable under the law. Defendant sought review. On appeal, the court found that defendant's contentions regarding inconsistent verdicts were without merit and that he was justly convicted. As to sentencing, the trial court was within its rights to consider defendant's involvement in the murder when sentencing. However, the court found that the sentence went beyond the maximum sentence provided by law by one year when the judge sentenced defendant under the larceny of the automobile conviction. The judgment was affirmed in part and vacated in part, and the case was remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part and vacated in part. The case was remanded to the court of special appeals for the entry of an order affirming the trial court judgment with respect to the conviction for receiving stolen goods and with respect to the conviction for larceny of an automobile, other than as to sentence. The case was to be remanded to the trial court for resentencing.

 

482 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Mangum v. Maryland State Board of Censors


No. 109, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


273 Md. 176; 328 A.2d 283; 1974 Md. LEXIS 699



November 25, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court of Baltimore City; Murphy, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed. Petitioner to pay costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner theater owner appealed an order from the Circuit Court of Baltimore City (Maryland), which upheld defendant state board of censors' refusal to grant a license for the exhibition of the film "Deep Throat."


OVERVIEW: Following the board's refusal to grant the owner a license to show the film, the owner sought review. The trial court entered an injunction, which restrained the owner from showing the film. The court ultimately dismissed the owner's appeal of the injunction. In the meantime, the trial court reviewed the evidence and issued a final order declaring that the film was obscene. On appeal, the court rejected the owner's contention that the trial court was deprived of jurisdiction to issue the final order by the injunction's appeal. The state had an interest in an expeditious decision, and an appeal of an interlocutory order did not deprive the trial court of jurisdiction. Upon review of the film, the court affirmed the categorization of it as obscene, hard-core pornography under the Miller test. The court reviewed the history of the obscenity classification and characterized the film as concentrating on genitalia and held that the brief satire served only as an introduction to sexually explicit scenes. The application of the Miller test did not violate the separation of powers under Md. Const. Decl. Rts. art. 8 because it was the judiciary's duty to construe legislation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the trial court and the board, which held that the film was obscene and refused the owner a license to exhibit the film.

Wilson v. Board of County Comm'rs


No. 146, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


273 Md. 30; 327 A.2d 488; 1974 Md. LEXIS 688; 7 ERC (BNA) 1289



November 7, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Allegany County; Getty, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed; appellants to pay the costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of the judgment from the Circuit Court of Allegany County (Maryland), which held that the revenue bonds proposed to be issued by appellee county commissioners in connection with pollution control measures to be installed by a private corporation were valid.


OVERVIEW: The state required a private corporation to construct pollution control facilities in conjunction with the corporation's plan to increase production and available job opportunities. Because the construction was the purpose of increasing economic development and employment opportunities within the county and state, the county commissioners agreed to issue bonds to fund the pollution control facilities portion of the project. The taxpayers objected to the issuance of the bonds. On appeal, the court confirmed the trial court's finding that the bonds were valid. The court determined that: (1) the pollution control facilities served a public purpose; (2) the funding of that portion of the project did not increase production or revenues for the corporation; (3) the parties and facilities met the definitions contained in Md. Ann. Code art. 41, § 266A; and (4) the bonds fit within the purpose and authority granted to the county in § 266B.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that the proposed revenue bonds were valid.

Hechinger Co. v. State's Attorney for Prince George's County


No. 31, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


272 Md. 706; 326 A.2d 742; 1974 Md. LEXIS 801

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant retailer appealed, by writ of certiorari, from a judgment of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which affirmed an order granting injunctive relief against the retailer from a petition filed by plaintiff State of Maryland seeking to enforce the Sunday closing law, Md. Ann. Code Art. 27, § 534H (1971), by prohibiting the retailer from operating its store on Sundays.


OVERVIEW: The retailer operated a store that sold a wide range of general merchandise, including lumber, plant materials, nursery-related items, appliances, and hardware. After it was discovered that the retailer's store was open for business on Sundays, the State's attorney filed a petition for injunctive relief to prohibit the retailer form operating its store on Sundays, claiming that the retailer was in violation of the Sunday closing law. The retailer alleged that its store was entitled to an exemption from the Sunday closing law, which was extended to nurseries, because the retailer sold plants and other nursery-related items. The circuit court ruled in favor of the State's attorney and ordered the retailer to cease from operating its store on Sunday, finding that the retailer was not a nursery. The retailer appealed, and the special court of appeals affirmed. On certiorari to the court, the court affirmed and ruled that the retailer was not entitled to an exemption form the Sunday closing law where its principal business was not that of a nursery because the retailer sold a wide range of general merchandise as well as plants and other nursery-related items.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the special appeals court, and the court vacated the stay order.

Wilson v. Board of Supervisors of Elections


No. 177, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


273 Md. 296; 328 A.2d 305; 1974 Md. LEXIS 707



October 24, 1974  
December 4, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Two appeals in one record from the Circuit Court of Baltimore City; Howard, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, taxpayer and Maryland Sports Complex Authority, appealed from a judgment of the Circuit Court of Baltimore City (Maryland), which sustained a demurrer by intervening defendants and denied plaintiffs' request for injunctive relief in plaintiffs' action to enjoin defendant members of the Board of Supervisors of Elections of Baltimore City from submitting to voters a proposed amendment to the city charter.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sought to enjoin defendants from submitting to voters a proposed amendment to the city charter, arguing that the amendment would prohibit the building of a sports stadium using public funds and would be in conflict with Md. Ann. Code art. 41, § 145(m), (n) (1957, 1971 Repl. Vol., 1974 Cum. Supp.) of the Maryland Sports Complex Authority Act, which authorized the building of a stadium with public funds in Baltimore. The court affirmed the denial of the injunction. The court applied the doctrine that if it was possible to construe the charter provision so as to avoid conflict with the United States Constitution or any public general law the court should do so. The court held that the effect of the amendment to the charter was only to prohibit construction in the city of such a stadium with funds of the city. Thus, the amendment was valid and operative.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sustaining of intervening defendants' demurrer and the denial of injunctive relief in the action to enjoin defendants from submitting to voters a proposed amendment to the city charter.

Maryland--National Capital Park & Planning Com. v. Rockville


No. 13, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


272 Md. 550; 325 A.2d 748; 1974 Md. LEXIS 792



October 8, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Joint Motion for Clarification or Rehearing Filed October 24, 1974; Denied October 24, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Shure, C. J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for passage of a decree conformable to the views expressed herein; appellees to pay costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, planning commission and county, sought review of the decision of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) which granted summary judgment in favor of appellees, city and mayor, in appellants' action for declaratory and injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: Appellants filed a bill of complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief against appellees arising out of the rezoning of annexed land. Upon remand after an appeal on preliminary issues, the parties filed motions for summary judgment. The trial court granted appellees' motion for summary judgment ruling that appellees' rezoning of the annexed land complied with 1971 Md. Laws ch. 116. On appeal, the court reversed the decision. The court held that the use permitted by the city's zoning classification was substantially different from the uses specified by any of the three zones in the county's master plans, which all provided for single family dwellings, due to the town houses and garden apartments to be developed under the planned residential unit development. The city's new zoning ordinance for the annexed land had to be compared to the master plans in effect prior to the annexation. Ch. 116 did not violate Md. Const. Decl. Rts. art. 23 because it was not so vague and indefinite as to be no standard at all. Ch. 116 did not violate the Due Process Clause because it was rationally related to a legitimate state objective and was not arbitrary or unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded the case so that judgment could be granted in appellants' favor and the appropriate declaratory and injunctive relief could be awarded in appellants' action arising out of the rezoning of annexed land.

State ex rel. Sonner v. Shearin


Misc. No. 3, No. 86, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


272 Md. 502; 325 A.2d 573; 1974 Md. LEXIS 789; 73 A.L.R.3d 454



October 1, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] In Misc. No. 3, petition for writ of mandamus, writ of certiorari or other appropriate relief.

In No. 86, appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Shearin, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: All pending motions denied; Misc. No. 3 dismissed; No. 86 remanded to the Circuit Court for Montgomery County for imposition of a proper sentence; costs in Misc. No. 3 to be paid by Montgomery County; costs in No. 86 to be paid by the defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a decision of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which denied the state's motion to correct the sentence the circuit court imposed on defendant for the use of a handgun in the commission of a felony. The state also filed a petition with the court for the writ of mandamus, the writ of certiorari, or other appropriate relief.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered guilty pleas to several charges, including use of a handgun in the commission of a felony. The trial court suspended a portion of a five-year prison sentence for the use of a handgun in the commission of a felony. In an attempt to compel the revision of the sentence, the state filed a petition with the court for the writ of mandamus, the writ of certiorari, or other appropriate relief. The court ordered that the petition should be held in abeyance pending the state's application to the trial court for an order nullifying the suspension of three years of the sentence. The trial court denied the state's motion to correct the sentence and the state's petition for mandamus. Defendant was allowed to intervene. On appeal, the court dismissed the state's petition for a writ and remanded the case to the circuit court for imposition of a proper sentence. The court held that if there was a conflict between Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 36B and Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 643, the General Assembly had mandated that Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 36B prevailed.Thus the trial court could not suspend defendant's sentence for the use of a handgun in the commission of a felony.


OUTCOME: The court denied all pending motions, dismissed the state's petition for a writ, and remanded the case to the circuit court to delete the improper suspension of sentence so that the sentence for the handgun violation would be for the mandatory term of five years.

In re Trader


Nos. 29, 32, 54, September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


272 Md. 364; 325 A.2d 398; 1974 Md. LEXIS 784



September 13, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Matter of Trader

Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland.

State v. Stokes

Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland.

Matter of Faulkner

Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland.

Matter of Thomas

Appeal from the Circuit Court for Kent County; Rasin, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: As to Matter of Trader: Judgment of the Court of Special Appeals affirming the dismissal of the indictments reversed and case remanded to the Criminal Court of Baltimore for further proceedings consistent with this opinion; costs to be paid by appellee. As to State v. Stokes: Judgment of the Criminal Court of Baltimore dismissing the indictment reversed and case remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion; costs to be paid by appellee. As to Matter of Faulkner: Appeal dismissed; costs to be paid by appellant. As to Matter of Thomas: Appeal dismissed; costs to be paid by appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of decisions in two separate cases by the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which dismissed indictments. In two other cases certiorari was granted to review the judgments prior to a decision by the Court of Special Appeals. All the cases involved offenses committed by appellee juveniles and were governed by one of two statutes, depending on the locality where the offense was committed.


OVERVIEW: Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. §§ 3-801 through 3-842 (1974) set forth the substantive law governing juvenile proceedings in Baltimore City and all of the counties except Montgomery County. Article §§ 4-501 through 4-530, a public local law, governed juvenile proceedings in Montgomery County. A juvenile who committed an offense in Montgomery County was subject to that county's juvenile law. A juvenile who committed an offense in Baltimore City or in any county other than Montgomery County was subject to the public general law. The court reversed the indictment dismissals in the first two cases, ruling one juvenile was not denied equal protection under the Fourteenth Amendment because pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 3-817, the waiver order of juvenile jurisdiction was interlocutory, and the other juvenile was not denied equal protection due to the statutory differences. The court granted the third juvenile's motion to dismiss, ruling the state's appeal was taken from an interlocutory order. The court granted the state's motion to dismiss the appeal of the fourth juvenile, ruling that under § 3-817, the order waiving juvenile jurisdiction was interlocutory.


OUTCOME: In the first case, the court reversed the indictment dismissal and remanded to the trial court. In the second case, the court reversed the indictment dismissal and remanded to the trial court. In the third case, the court granted the juvenile's motion to dismiss. In the fourth case, the court granted the state's motion to dismiss.

State Administrative Board of Election Laws v. Calvert


No. 133 (Adv.), September Term, 1974


Court of Appeals of Maryland


272 Md. 659; 327 A.2d 290; 1974 Md. LEXIS 798



August 23, 1974  
October 10, 1974, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Cecil County; Mackey, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Board of Supervisors of Elections for Cecil County, the Maryland State Administrative Board of Election Laws, and the Maryland State Administrator of Election Laws, sought review of a judgment entered in the Circuit Court for Cecil County (Maryland), which enjoined appellants from limiting the names of candidates in a primary election ballot. Appellee candidate challenged a residency requirement in the legislative district.


OVERVIEW: The board was enjoined from eliminating the names of unopposed candidates from the primary ballot and ordered to list all candidates who sought nomination for election to the Maryland House of Delegates, which would have allowed the voters to vote for three candidates. The candidate contended that the requirement imposed an additional restriction on the eligibility of some candidates and violated the one-man one-vote principle. The court reversed, concluding that the circuit court was without jurisdiction, under Md. Const. art. III, § 5, when it ruled that the legislative districting plan was unconstitutional. The court overruled its opinion in Secretary of State v. Bryson, 244 Md. 418, 224 A.2d 277 (1966) and concluded that there was a legitimate state interest in scattering delegates throughout the district so that no voter was far removed from a delegate. It also held that a county resident who sought the Democratic delegate nomination should not have had his name placed on the primary election ballot under Md. Ann. Code art. 33, § 5-3 (c) (1957, Repl. Vol. 1971).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order as to the constitutionality of the legislative district and declared that no county within the legislative district was to have more than one delegate residing therein.

Bureau of Mines v. George's Creek Coal & Land Co.


No. 261, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


272 Md. 143; 321 A.2d 748; 1974 Md. LEXIS 771; 7 ERC (BNA) 1001; 86 A.L.R.3d 1; 4 ELR 20822



July 3, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motion for Rehearing Filed July 31, 1974; Denied July 31, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Garrett County; Hamill, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Case remanded without affirmance or reversal pursuant to the provisions of Maryland Rule 871 a for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion; costs to abide the result.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, Bureau of Mines of Maryland (bureau), appealed the decision of the Circuit Court of Garrett County (Maryland), which held that plaintiffs, holding company and mine company, should have been allowed to continue their strip mining operations. A substantial property right was involved. Since there was no compensation paid by the bureau for this property taking, the bureau was required to continue the mining company's permit.


OVERVIEW: The bureau challenged the district court's decision that held that the bureau could not stop the mining company from continuing to strip mine certain property in which the holding company had mineral and mining rights. The land was within a state forest. On appeal, the court remanded the case for further proceedings. The court was faced with the question of whether a state law banning strip mining on state-owned lands was a lawful exercise of the police power or a taking of private property for public purposes, requiring payment of just compensation to the owners of the mineral rights. The court held that the statutory prohibition of strip mining constituted a reasonable regulation under the state's police power to protect the environment and to preserve state-owned land for public use. The prohibition of strip mining was reasonably necessary to accomplish the legislative purpose. The case was remanded to determine whether the strip mining ban was unduly oppressive to the mining company or the holding company.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case for further proceedings to determine whether the bureau's prohibition of strip mining was oppressive to the mining company or the holding company.

Baltimore Country Club, Inc. v. Comptroller of Treasury


No. 249, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


272 Md. 65; 321 A.2d 308; 1974 Md. LEXIS 765



June 24, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Maryland Tax Court; Korn, C. J., and Logan, Hughes and Arthur, JJ.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed; costs to be paid by appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff country club challenged a judgment entered by the Maryland Tax Court, which affirmed a decision rendered by defendant Comptroller of the Treasury (Comptroller) that levied a sales tax upon the country club's mandatory service charge that it added to its members' food and beverage bills.


OVERVIEW: After it computed a member's food and beverage bill, and added the state sales tax to the bill, the country club added an additional 15 per cent of the menu price and the sales tax as a mandatory service or gratuity charge. The Comptroller contended that the charge was a part of the purchase price, as defined by the Maryland Retail Sales Tax Act, Md. Ann. Code art. 81, § 324 et seq. (1969 Repl. Vol.). The country club argued that the service charge should not be taxed because it received no benefit from its imposition or collection. In affirming the tax court, the court held that the mandatory 15 per cent service charge was part of the "price" of the country club's retail sales of food and beverages and as such was subject to the sales tax. The court also held that the imposition of the sales tax on the mandatory charge did not violate equal protection principles even though a member's voluntary tip to a waiter was not taxable. The court stated that there was a constitutionally reasonable distinction for sales tax purposes between voluntary and mandatory gratuities.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the tax court, which held that mandatory service charges on member's food and beverage bills, which the country club collected and paid over to its employees, were subject to the state's retail sales tax.

Dart Drug Corp. v. Hechinger Co.


No. 298, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


272 Md. 15; 320 A.2d 266; 1974 Md. LEXIS 759



June 3, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Three appeals in one record from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Shure, C. J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Decree modified as set forth in the foregoing opinion, and as modified, affirmed. Mandate to issue forthwith. Stay heretofore granted vacated upon issuance of mandate. Costs to be paid by appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated action, defendant drug stores appealed three decisions from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which granted plaintiff hardware store's claims for declaratory and injunctive relief to enforce the Sunday closing law, Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 534J, against the drug stores.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held the statute to be constitutional. Although the drug stores sold prescription drugs, this comprised less than half of their business. Thus, they were not entitled to the exemption for drug stores whose basic business was the sale of drugs and related items. While the statute was a criminal statute, the hardware store had standing, under the Uniform Declaratory Judgments Act, Md. Ann. Code, art. 31A, § 6, to seek an injunction to compel compliance. The court found no abuse of discretion in denying the drug stores' demurrer for failing to join necessary parties. There was no information on other drug stores, listed in the phone book but not joined, as to whether these stores had more than 6 employees per shift, or if they were open on Sundays. It also found no abuse in discretion in failing to grant the drug stores' eleventh hour request for a continuance. Finally, the court held the trial court properly permitted the hardware store to amend its complaint to correct the corporate name of one of the drug store chains. However, the court found that the drug stores should have been permitted to remain open to sell the exempt drugs and related items.


OUTCOME: The court modified the decree to permit the drug stores to sell drugs and related items, and then affirmed the decision as modified.

Billingsley v. Lincoln Nat'l Bank


No. 228, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


271 Md. 683; 320 A.2d 34; 1974 Md. LEXIS 1071



May 29, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Cahoon, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed; appellants to pay costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant endorsers challenged a decision of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which denied the endorsers' motion to vacate a judgment by confession entered against them, plus attorney fees and court costs, on a promissory note in favor of appellee bank.


OVERVIEW: A promissory note was executed by a corporation in Philadelphia, Pennsylvania that authorized the confession and entry of judgment against the maker in favor of the bank at any time after it became due. The endorsers signed the note and also expressly guaranteed payment and submitted to the authorization for confession of judgment. After a default by the maker and an unsuccessful demand for payment upon the endorsers, the bank filed an action. The endorsers attacked the constitutionality of Maryland Rule 645 on grounds that it violated the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV because it permitted attachment and execution of property before notice. The court held that Rule 645 was facially constitutional because the debtors were given every opportunity after judgment to present any defenses that they may have had and were merely required to meet a standard that was a minimal obstacle. The court held that Rule 645 was constitutional as applied. The court held that a waiver hearing prior to entry of a confessed judgment was not required and, if one was required thereafter, it was clearly available at the hearing on the endorsers' motion to vacate, and no more was needed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court in favor of the bank that denied the endorsers' motion to vacate a judgment by confession entered against them. The endorsers were to pay costs.

State Dep't of Assessments & Taxation v. Greyhound Computer Corp.


No. 205, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


271 Md. 575; 320 A.2d 40; 1974 Md. LEXIS 1061



May 7, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motion for Rehearing Filed June 6 and 10, 1974; Denied June 19, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Five appeals in one record from the Maryland Tax Court; Korn, C. J., and Logan, Blair, Hughes and Arthur, JJ.



DISPOSITION: Case remanded to the Maryland Tax Court without affirmance or reversal, pursuant to the provisions of Maryland Rule 871 a, for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion; costs to be paid one-half by the appellant and one-half by appellees.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Department of Assessments and Taxation sought review of the judgment of the Maryland Tax Court, which found that the department had violated Md. Const. Decl. Rts. art. 15 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV by instituting a new standard of valuation based on "selling price" for equipment manufactured and leased by appellee manufacturers, while applying the established standard based on cost to the taxpayer for other personal property.


OVERVIEW: The manufacturers leased copy equipment and were taxed based on the cost of manufacture. Appellee non-manufacturers leased copy equipment and were taxed based on the sales price of the equipment. The tax court first found that the manufacturers and non-manufacturers could not be taxed at different rates and then found that the manufacturers could not be taxed based on the sales price. The court remanded without affirmance or reversal, finding that (1) the leased property was not "stock in business" per Md. Ann. Code art. 81 §§ 14(a)(1) and 15(a), and was not "distilled spirits," and therefore fell in the category of other personal property; (2) other personal property was to be taxed at its full cash value, which meant the market value; (3) the manufacturers had to be taxed at the full cash value; and (4) the department did not deny the manufacturers due process of law. The court remanded for a determination of whether the department's policy of assessing the property of the "manufacturer-lessors" at its full cash value was, or was not, applied to taxable tangible personal property manufactured but not leased by its owner.


OUTCOME: The court remanded for a determination of whether the department's policy of assessing the property of the "manufacturer-lessors" at its full cash value was, or was not, applied to taxable tangible personal property manufactured but not leased by its owner.

Gaithersburg v. Montgomery County


No. 212, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


271 Md. 505; 318 A.2d 509; 1974 Md. LEXIS 1053



May 1, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Mathias, J., pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed. Appellants to pay costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, Montgomery County (Maryland), members of the Montgomery City Council (Maryland), and a state park commission, brought an action for declaratory judgment and injunctive relief against appellants, City of Gaithersburg (Maryland) and property owners, regarding the annexation and rezoning of the owners' land. The Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) entered a decree for appellees. Appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: After the effective date of 1971 Md. Laws 116, the City annexed a parcel that included the owners' land. 1971 Md. Laws 116 added a clause to Md. Ann. Code art. 23A, § 9(c) (1973) to provide that no municipality annexing land could, within a five-year period, rezone the land so as to permit a use substantially different from that specified in the county's master plan. The City and the owners defended appellees' action on the sold ground that 1971 Md. Laws 116 violated Md. Const. art. XI-E. The circuit court upheld the constitutionality of the statute and declared the rezoning to be null and void. On appeal, appellants contended, among other things, that 1971 Md. Laws 116 violated Md. Const. art. XI-E, § 1, which required that legislation must apply alike to all municipalities in a class. The court held that, given the plain meaning of 1971 Md. Laws 116 and its legislative history, 1971 Md. Laws 116 did not violate Md. Const. art. XI-E, § 1 because its enactment was a limitation upon the home rule powers of all municipalities subject to Md. Const. art. XI-E, § 1 even though the commission only operated in two specific counties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decree.

In re Foster


Misc. (Judicial Disabilities) No. 1, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


271 Md. 449; 318 A.2d 523; 1974 Md. LEXIS 1052



April 29, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Findings, Conclusions and Recommendations of the Commission on Judicial Disabilities.



DISPOSITION: For the reasons set forth, it is this 29th day of April, 1974 by the Court of Appeals of Maryland, ordered that the recommendation of the Commission on Judicial Disabilities be, and it is hereby, accepted; and it is further ordered that Judge Dulany Foster be, and he is hereby, censured.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judge challenged the findings of the Commission on Judicial Disabilities (Maryland), which concluded that the judge's actions, with respect to the purchase and development of a tract of land, created an appearance of impropriety. The Commission recommended the judge's censure.


OVERVIEW: The judge contended that there could be no appearance of impropriety because there was no indication that the concessions granted to the judge would not have been accorded to any other responsible developer. The court held that the test was not whether the judge, by reason of his position and prestige, was able to achieve a result which another might not have accomplished, but rather whether a reasonable man would have been justified in suspecting that the result which the judge achieved was achieved because of his position and prestige. The court adopted the recommendations and ordered the judge censured, holding that based on our independent review of the record, the finding that the judge's actions created the appearance of impropriety was supported by clear and convincing evidence. The court held that when the judge personally and publicly assumed a command responsibility in the furtherance of the real estate project, he embarked on a course likely to collide with the prohibition contained in the Maryland Code of Judicial Ethics.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the recommendation of the Commission was accepted and that the judge was censured.

 

497 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. State


No. 178, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


271 Md. 189; 315 A.2d 524; 1974 Md. LEXIS 1033



February 28, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which dismissed his appeal from the trial court in which defendant contended that he had an absolute right of removal to another jurisdiction under Md. Const. art. IV, § 8.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted by a grand jury for rape, assault with intent to rape, and assault. On his trial date, defendant filed in writing and under oath a suggestion that he could not obtain a fair and impartial trial in Baltimore, Maryland, and he requested the case be removed to another jurisdiction. Defendant set forth no supporting facts or reasons but claimed an absolute right of removal under art. IV, § 8. The trial court denied defendant's request for removal. The appellate court dismissed defendant's appeal. The court granted certiorari and affirmed the appellate court's order dismissing the appeal. The court held that art. IV, § 8 gave a defendant an absolute right of removal only if he was charged with an offense that was "punishable by death." In such a case, defendant had to do no more than file a suggestion under oath that he could not receive a fair trial. However, where the crime was not punishable by death, defendant had to go further and make it satisfactorily appear that the suggestion was true or that there was a reasonable ground. Rape or assault with intent to rape were not offenses punishable by death, and defendant had no absolute right of removal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision that correctly dismissed defendant's appeal from the trial court's order denying his request for removal.

 

498 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Annapolis v. Anne Arundel County


No. 184, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


271 Md. 265; 316 A.2d 807; 1974 Md. LEXIS 1037



January 30, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motion for Rehearing Filed February 28, 1974; After Hearing, Opinion Confirmed Without Change March 18, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Five appeals in one record from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County; Weant and Wray, JJ. pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Decree of September 5, 1973, reversed and the case remanded to the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County for the entry of a decree declaring that the appellee, Anne Arundel County, is subject to the application of the Historic District Ordinance, sections 22-226 through 22-236, inclusive, of the code of the City of Annapolis and making permanent the injunction issued on September 6, 1973, enjoining the appellee, Anne Arundel County, from proceeding with the demolition of the building known as Mt. Moriah African Methodist Episcopal Church of Annapolis; the costs to be paid by the appellee, Anne Arundel County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city and its historical commission sought to enjoin appellee county from demolishing a historical church and sought a declaration that the county was required to comply with the city's historic district ordinance. Following a hearing on the merits, the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland) denied the city's request for an injunction, but ruled against the county on other issues. The parties cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The county argued that the actions of the historical commission, which ruled prior to the trial court action, that the county could not demolish the church to build a parking facility, had deprived the county of its property without due process of law and that the property was taken for public use without just compensation. The court held that the county was subject to the jurisdiction of the historical commission. In so holding, the court found that 1) the chancellor properly held that the trial court properly ruled that the city code, which provided an automatic approval of the county's demolition application if no decision was reached within 45 days, did not apply because the time for the commission action was extended by mutual agreement; 2) the trial court erred in ruling that the county was not subject to the commission's jurisdiction; 3) the county failed to establish how it intended to develop the disputed site in connection with its courthouse; 4) the legislature intended that historic zoning to be different from traditional zoning; and 5) the denial of the county's demolition application did not constitute a "taking" of the county's property.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's ruling and remanded the case to the trial court for the entry of a decree declaring that the county was subject to the application of the city's historic district ordinance, and a permanent injunction prohibiting the county from demolishing the church.

 

499 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Baltimore County v. Churchill, Ltd.


No. 116, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


271 Md. 1; 313 A.2d 829; 1974 Md. LEXIS 1018



January 10, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore County; Proctor, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, costs to be paid by appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action brought by appellants, political subdivisions and their officials, for a declaratory judgment that Md. Ann. Code art. 81, § 39A(1973) was unconstitutional and to enjoin hearings on refunds claimed by appellee taxpayers, the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) granted the taxpayers' summary judgment motions and declared that the statute was a valid and constitutional enactment. Appellants sought review of the order.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers sought refunds of personal property taxes paid as a result of erroneous assessments. The taxpayers alleged that appellants lacked standing to bring the action. The court held that, although the political subdivisions lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of Md. Ann. Code art. 81, § 39A(1973), the individual public officials had standing. Appellants alleged that the act violated the United States Constitution and the Maryland Constitution. The court held that the statute was not a special law as contemplated by Md. Const. art. III, § 33 that prohibited special laws to refund money paid into the state treasury. The court held that Article 15 of the Maryland Declaration of Rights that requires uniformity in the assessment and taxation of land was inapplicable because Article 15 had nothing to do with refunds. The court held that the legislature had the power to grant an exemption retroactively. The court held that appellants had no vested right in taxes that were collected in error. Because state taxes refunds are matters of legislative grace, the statute was a proper exercise of the power of the General Assembly.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's orders that granted the taxpayers' motions for summary judgment and that declared that the statute was a valid and constitutional exercise of the power of the state legislature.

A. H. Smith Sand & Gravel Co. v. Department of Water Resources


No. 102, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


270 Md. 652; 313 A.2d 820; 1974 Md. LEXIS 1343



January 4, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Two appeals in one record from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County; Taylor, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed; costs to be divided between the parties.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant gravel company sought review of an order of the Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland), which approved of a decision of appellee Department of Water Resources requiring that no filling operations were to occur within the 50-year floodplain boundaries on the company's land without obtaining a permit from the department. The department also appealed the trial court's order insofar as it modified the floodplain's boundaries.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the company claimed error because the trial judge approved that portion of an order of the department that required no filling operations were to take place within the 50-year floodplain boundaries on the company's land without obtaining a permit from the department for such activities. The court found no error. The court did not agree that the company was being denied use of its property and that its property was being appropriated without compensation. To determine whether the plan as it affected the company's land was valid, it had to first exhaust its administrative remedies by making application for a permit, since upon a proper application a record would have been developed. With that record before it, a court would have been in a position to decide whether the department in applying that general statutory plan in the company's particular situation infringed upon its constitutional rights. At this point the court had no adequate record to make such a determination. On appeal, the department claimed the trial judge erred when he modified its order by changing the boundaries of the floodplain as determined by the department. The trial judge did not err in the change.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in either the approval of the decision requiring that no filling operations were to occur within the 50-year floodplain boundaries on the company's land without obtaining a permit from the department nor in the modification of the floodplain's boundaries.

Maryland State Board of Barber Examiners v. Kuhn


No. 90, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


270 Md. 496; 312 A.2d 216; 1973 Md. LEXIS 700



December 5, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court of Baltimore City; Perrott, J.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed; appellants to pay costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff cosmetologists brought suit against defendant board of barber examiners (board) seeking declaratory and injunctive relief. The cosmetologists challenged the constitutionality of a Md. Ann. Code art. 43, § 529, which permitted barbers to cut the hair of both men and women, but restricted cosmetologists to cutting the hair of women. The Circuit Court of Baltimore City (Maryland) entered a judgment in favor of the cosmetologists.


OVERVIEW: The trial court concluded that there was no intrinsic physical difference between hair on a man's head and hair on a women's head. Therefore, the trial court struck down Md. Ann. Code art. 43, § 529, as violative of the Fourteenth Amendment and article 56 of the Declaration of Rights. The court determined that the trial court properly determined that the statute could not be construed favorably to the cosmetologists without a resolution of the constitutional issues. The court found that this case did not involve discrimination based on sex under the Fourteenth Amendment or Article 56; therefore, the statute was not subject to strict scrutiny. The court determined that § 529 was not rationally related to a legitimate state purpose. The cosmetologists also contended that § 529 deprived them of a legal occupation without due process of law. The board contended that § 529 was a reasonable exercise of police power, and thus did not result in a denial of due process. However, the court determined that the statute was unreasonable and arbitrary and held that it did deprive the cosmetologists of due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of the cosmetologists, which determined that a state statute limiting a cosmetologists work to women clients only was unconstitutional.

 

502 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Lucky Stores, Inc. v. Board of Appeals


No. 100, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


270 Md. 513; 312 A.2d 758; 1973 Md. LEXIS 701



December 5, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Moorman, J.



DISPOSITION: Motion to dismiss the appeal denied; order of April 6, 1973, affirmed, the appellant to pay the costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant store sought review of an order from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which affirmed a decision made by appellee Board of Appeals of Montgomery Count that denied the store's application for a special zoning exception for a gasoline filing station. The board filed a motion to dismiss the appeal.


OVERVIEW: The store filed an application with the board for a special zoning exception for the construction of a gas filling station. The board denied the store's application and the trial court affirmed. Upon review, the court affirmed the denial of the store's application and denied the board's motion to dismiss the store's appeal. The court held a zoning ordinance that provided special exceptions could only be granted upon a showing that a public need existed for the proposed use was not unconstitutional where the ordinance was not designed to prevent competition. The court determined that there was a reasonable basis for the denial of the store's application where the evidence indicated there were 5 filing stations nearby and that there were 20 other filing stations within a distance of 3.8 miles of the proposed site of the gas station. The court found there were two discount gas operations already in existence in the marketing area and that the need for a discount operation was already being provided for. The court concluded that the case did not become moot by the store erecting a building on the proposed site because the store could have still pursued obtaining a permit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order that upheld the board's denial of the store's application for a special zoning exception for a gasoline station. The court denied the board's motion to dismiss the appeal and ordered the store to pay costs.

 

503 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


County Council for Montgomery County v. Investors Funding Corp.


No. 282, September Term, 1972


Court of Appeals of Maryland


270 Md. 403; 312 A.2d 225; 1973 Md. LEXIS 697



December 4, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Two appeals in one record from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Moore, J.



DISPOSITION: Decree vacated; case remanded for the entry of a decree consistent with this opinion; costs to be paid one-half by the County Council for Montgomery County and one-half by Investors Funding Corporation et al. 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlords and appellee county council sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which found that certain provisions of a bill enacted by appellee were illegal, unconstitutional, or otherwise nugatory.


OVERVIEW: Appellant landlords sought a declaratory decree that a bill enacted by appellee county council was void. The lower court concluded that various provisions of the bill were in conflict with the public general laws of the state. On appeal by both parties, the court vacated the decree and remanded for a new decree. A provision against retaliatory eviction was unlawful because the public general laws granted a landlord a legal right to evict, and appellee could not require him to agree not to do so. A provision requiring two-year terms for leases and a provision as to confession of judgment were not in conflict with public general laws. Oral leases were permitted by the public general law and could not be prohibited by appellee. Because of the complete lack of any legislative safeguards or standards, the grant of unlimited discretion to a county administrative body to fix civil penalties in any amount up to $ 1,000 was illegal. All other remedial powers vested in this body by the ordinance were legal and constitutional. An amended section making the right to a hearing absolute and not discretionary fully comported with procedural due process requirements.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the decree that declared that various provisions of a bill enacted by appellee county council and challenged by appellant landlords were in conflict with the public general laws of the state. Provisions against retaliatory eviction and oral leases were unlawful as in conflict with public general laws, but provisions as to two-year terms and confession of judgment were not in conflict. The cause was remanded for a new decree.

Baltimore v. William E. Koons, Inc.


No. 49, September Term, 1973


Court of Appeals of Maryland


270 Md. 231; 310 A.2d 813; 1973 Md. LEXIS 680



November 8, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Two appeals in one record from the Circuit Court of Baltimore City; Perrott, J.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both parties appealed an order of the Circuit Court of Baltimore City (Maryland), which declared appellants, city and housing commissioner, "technically in contempt" of its earlier decree and declared an amended city regulation null and void, but which did not award an attorney's fee to appellee property owners' counsel.


OVERVIEW: Buildings belonging to the property owners contained unvented gas-fired space heaters. They were threatened with prosecution by the city if they refused to remove the heaters, which violated Baltimore, Md., Amended Reg. 5 (1971). The trial court declared the predecessor regulation unconstitutional in a previous action involving the same parties and enjoined the city from its enforcement. The trial court found the amended regulation was void because it contravened the meaning of the Baltimore, Md., City Housing Code, §§ 612, 614 (1966). It also found the city and commissioner "technically in contempt" of its earlier decree, but did not award an attorney's fee to the property owners. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court and held that the city could not prohibit by regulation that which was permitted by the Housing Code. The court vacated the contempt part of the decree because the property owners admitted that their purpose in bringing the action was to continue the previous suit. The court refused to award an attorney's fee to the property owners because the city had made a good faith attempt to restructure it rule consistent with the trial court's earlier decree.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court except for the part adjudicating the city and commissioner in contempt of court. The court did not award an attorney's fee to the property owners.
