Cohen v. Governor of Maryland


No. 120 (Adv.), September Term, 1969


Court of Appeals of Maryland


255 Md. 5; 255 A.2d 320; 1969 Md. LEXIS 678



June 30, 1969  
July 18, 1969, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court of Baltimore City; Carter, Joseph L., J.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen appealed the decision of the Circuit Court of Baltimore City (Maryland), which entered judgment dismissing the citizen's complaint challenging the constitutionality of 1969 Md. Laws ch. 76. Chapter 76 purportedly provided for a "general election" for submission to the electorate of all amendments to the Maryland Constitution proposed by the Maryland General Assembly at its 1969 session.


OVERVIEW: During its 1969 session, the Maryland General Assembly passed 1969 Md. Laws ch. 76. Chapter 76 provided for a "general election" for the submission of all amendments proposed during the 1969 session to the electorate for adoption or rejection. The citizen filed a complaint alleging that the "general election" scheduled for November 4, 1969 was not a "general election" as required by Md. Const. art. XIV, § 1 but was a "special election" convened specifically to vote on the proposed amendments. The citizen's complaint sought judgment declaring 1969 Md. Laws ch. 76 unconstitutional or constitutional as well as a decision on the constitutionality of the eight proposed amendments. The complaint also sought injunction to prevent the election on November 4, 1969 from proceeding. The trial court dismissed the complaint. On appeal, the court held that the election scheduled was not a "general election" but a "special election." The court found that general elections referred to regularly scheduled elections in which various local and national candidates were elected to office, while the November 1969 election was more similar to a special referendum election.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment.

Baltimore v. Sitnick


Nos. 295, 297, September Term, 1968


Court of Appeals of Maryland


254 Md. 303; 255 A.2d 376; 1969 Md. LEXIS 874



June 27, 1969, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motions for Rehearing Filed July 25 and 28, 1969; Denied September 8, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: Consolidated appeals from the Superior Court of Baltimore City and the Circuit Court of Baltimore City; Sklar, J.



DISPOSITION: Orders reversed, cases remanded for the passage of a judgment declaring the city minimum wage ordinances to be valid. Appellees to pay costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, mayor and city council (city), brought consolidated appeals of the judgment of the Superior Court of Baltimore City and the Circuit Court of Baltimore City (Maryland), which overruled the city's demurrers and rendered declaratory judgments in favor of plaintiffs, hotel and bar owners, that found that the Baltimore Minimum Wage Law, Baltimore, Md., City Charter art. 19, §§ 51- 61 (1966), was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: After the city passed an ordinance establishing a higher minimum wage than had been established by the state, the hotel and bar owners filed declaratory actions against the city to have the ordinance declared void under Md. Const. art. XI-A, § 3. The trial court entered judgment in favor of the hotel and bar owners, and the city appealed. The court reversed, holding that the ordinance fell under the concurrent power theory because it did not conflict with state law or the state's legislative powers. Under that theory, additional regulation by a local government to that of state law did not constitute a conflict by enlarging upon the provisions of the statute or requiring more than the statute. The city could not prohibit what the state by general public law had permitted, but the city was allowed to prohibit what the state had not expressly permitted. The court examined the use of the preemption theory by other jurisdictions and disregarded it under the facts of the case because there was no showing that the state legislature intended to reserve to itself the exclusive right to legislate on the subject matter.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the hotel and bar owners' declaratory actions against the city and ordered the passage of judgments in favor of the city declaring that the Baltimore Minimum Wage Law was valid.

Bowie v. Board of County Comm'rs


No. 21 (Adv.), September Term, 1969


Court of Appeals of Maryland


253 Md. 602; 253 A.2d 727; 1969 Md. LEXIS 992



May 27, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Howard County; Macgill, C. J.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed. Costs to be paid by appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners sought review of a decree of the Circuit Court for Howard County (Maryland), which affirmed a decision by appellee board of county commissioners (commissioners) rezoning land for the construction of an industrial plant.


OVERVIEW: The developer granted the corporation an option to purchase land for the construction of an industrial plant. The corporation refused to exercise the option unless the land was reclassified as an industrial development district. The developer filed a petition for rezoning. After a public hearing, the commissioners granted the petition. The landowners filed an action challenging the commissioners' decision. The trial court entered a decree affirming the decision of the commissioners. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decree. The court held that land in question was not part of a floating zone, so the commissioners were required to make findings as to whether there had been a change in conditions since the adoption of the comprehensive zoning map or a mistake in zoning as to the land. The court determined that the commissioners had not specifically made such findings, but that the evidence showed that much of the land had been reclassified since the adoption of the map. Therefore, the court ruled that the commissioners' decision was "fairly debatable" and could not be reversed. The court determined that the evidence was sufficient to justify the rezoning.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decree upholding the commissioners' decision to rezone the land as an industrial development district.

Public Service Com. v. Hahn Transp., Inc.


No. 188, September Term, 1968


Court of Appeals of Maryland


253 Md. 571; 253 A.2d 845; 1969 Md. LEXIS 991



May 27, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court of Baltimore City and the Superior Court of Baltimore City; Cullen, J.



DISPOSITION: Orders reversed, with costs, and cases remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Baltimore City (Maryland) that declared a rule adopted by appellant Public Service Commission invalid. The rule forbade laymen to practice or appear in a representative capacity at a formal hearing, pursuant to Conn. Code art 78, § 62.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Public Service Commission adopted a rule forbidding laymen to practice or appear before it in a representative capacity in connection with or at a formal hearing, under the authority of Conn. Code art. 78, § 62. Appellee filed two petitions seeking a declaration that the rule was invalid. The two cases were consolidated and the trial court held that the rule was invalid. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that appellant had the power to require that an attorney must represent parties before it other than individuals who desire to act for themselves. When an individual on behalf of his employer or another prepares and files pleadings in a contested case before appellant and appears at and engages in trial by the examination or cross-examination of witnesses, he engages in the practice of law. Appellant had the power to require that those who engage in the practice of law before it be legally be authorized to practice law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment of the trial court that declared a rule adopted by appellant Public Service Commission invalid, holding that appellant had the authority to require that an attorney must represent parties that appeared before appellant. Those who engaged in the practice of law before appellant were required to be legally authorized to practice law.

Balenson v. Maryland Airport Authority


No. 374, September Term, 1968


Court of Appeals of Maryland


253 Md. 490; 251 A.2d 870; 1969 Md. LEXIS 983



May 9, 1969, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County; Childs, J. April 8, 1969, Per Curiam Order filed.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Taxpayer appealed from an order of the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland) dismissing his complaint for declaratory relief and permanent injunction against state and local authorities, alleging Md. Code Ann. art. 62C, § 7 (1957, 1968 Repl. Vol., 1968 Supp.) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Taxpayer challenged the constitutionality of a statute creating the Maryland Airport Authority. The state maintained that the statute was constitutional because it must be assumed that payments were to be made by the Authority. The city contended that the transaction was unconstitutional because the state was not obligated to make the payments. Amicus curiae argued that the constitutional issue would be moot if the governor signed a bill recently passed by the legislature. The court held that the state constitution required that a debt must be authorized by a law providing for the collection of an annual tax in an amount sufficient to pay the interest and to discharge the principal. The court held the act unconstitutional because it created a debt greater in amount than the general obligation bonds which were authorized, only part of which would have been available for the purchase of the airport.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the complaint, holding that the statute was invalid under the Maryland constitution, because no tax was provided for in the bill that authorized the debt.

Montgomery Citizens League v. Greenhalgh


No. 339, September Term, 1968


Court of Appeals of Maryland


253 Md. 151; 252 A.2d 242; 1969 Md. LEXIS 952

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens' league sought review of an order from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland) that sustained a demurrer to a bill and a demurrer to the amended bill without leave to amend in an action brought by the citizens' league that alleged the invalidity of a fair housing law and sought to enjoin appellee county council, executive secretary, three members of the commission's panel, and the county attorney from enforcing it.


OVERVIEW: After a prior decision held that the council could enact laws only when sitting in legislative session and, therefore, that a fair housing law enacted when the council was sitting in executive session was invalid and of no effect, the council, sitting in emergency legislative session, again enacted a fair housing law prohibiting discrimination. The citizens' league filed a bill alleged the law's invalidity and the circuit court judge sustained a demurrer to the bill and a demurrer to the amended bill without leave to amend. The court held that a fair housing or equal accommodation law was a prima facie reasonable exercise in good faith of the police power to protect the peace and good order of the community, and to promote its welfare and good government.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the circuit court in favor of appellees that sustained a demurrer to a bill and a demurrer to the amended bill without leave to amend in an action brought by the citizens' league that alleged the invalidity of a fair housing law.

 

595 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Cecil County Board of Education v. Pursley


No. 291, September Term, 1968


Court of Appeals of Maryland


252 Md. 672; 251 A.2d 205; 1969 Md. LEXIS 1129



March 12, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Cecil County; Rollins, J.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, the costs to be paid by the appellees.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board of education sought review of an interlocutory injunction entered by the Circuit Court for Cecil County (Maryland) in favor of appellees, parents and taxpayers. The parents and taxpayers sought to enjoin the board from transporting their children by school buses to a different school. The children lived approximately one mile from their former school. The trial court had overruled the board's demurrer to the bill of complaint.


OVERVIEW: The children were transferred as a part of a redistricting plan. Some of the children lived within a one-mile radius of their former school. At a hearing, there was no testimony whatever that the board had acted fraudulently, corruptly, or had abused its discretion so as to amount to a breach of trust. The court was of the opinion that under the applicable law, the evidence before the lower court was not sufficient to warrant the issuance of the interlocutory injunction, so the order was reversed. Md. Ann. Code art. 77, § 56 (1957), committed the districting and redistricting of the county for school purposes to the discretion of the board. The parents and taxpayers had the burden of showing that the board's action was illegal or an abuse of discretion, and that burden was not met. The trial court affirmatively found that the action by the board was not fraudulent, corrupt, or such an abuse of discretion as would amount to a breach of trust. There was no evidence before the trial judge to indicate that the board had acted fraudulently, corruptly, arbitrarily, unreasonably or capriciously in breach of its trust and, hence, the interlocutory injunction was erroneously issued.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order.

Grimm v. County Comm'rs of Washington County


No. 137, September Term, 1968


Court of Appeals of Maryland


252 Md. 626; 250 A.2d 866; 1969 Md. LEXIS 1123



March 10, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Washington County; Rutledge, J.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, the appellant to pay the costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff junk yard owner appealed the decision of the Circuit Court for Washington County (Maryland), which denied his petition in equity against the county commissioners under § 29 of the Unif. Declaratory Judgment Act, Md. Ann. Code art. 31A, § 2 (1957) for a declaration that the Washington County, Md., Junk Yard Ordinance, was unconstitutional and void.


OVERVIEW: The junk yard owner filed a petition in equity against the county commissioners under § 29 of the Unif. Declaratory Judgment Act seeking a declaration that the Washington County, Md., Junk Yard Ordinance, was unconstitutional and void and an sought injunction. The trial court dismissed the case holding that all issues could be decided in a pending criminal proceeding against the junk yard owner for violations of the ordinance. The junk yard owner appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision. The trial court did not abuse his discretion in directing the county commissioners to file a supplemental answer and in considering the issue of the proper applicability of declaratory relief procedures in the instant case because there was no surprise to the junk yard owner. The trial court had judicial discretion to refuse a declaratory judgment if it did not serve a useful purpose or terminate controversy. Once a criminal proceeding had been instituted in which the question of constitutionality of the ordinance could be adequately decided, the trial court properly exercised his discretion in declining to grant declaratory relief in the case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court dismissing the junkyard owner's petition in equity for declaratory and injunctive relief under the Uniform Declaratory Judgment Act.

 

597 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Carl M. Freeman Associates, Inc. v. State Roads Com.


No. 17, September Term, 1968


Court of Appeals of Maryland


252 Md. 319; 250 A.2d 250; 1969 Md. LEXIS 1092



February 7, 1969, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motions for Rehearing Filed March 7 and 10, 1969; Denied March 11, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County; Levine, J.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed with costs and case remanded for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of an order from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland). The trial court, over the objections of the property owner in a condemnation proceedings, admitted into evidence testimony as to the value of a 7.94 acre parcel of land being condemned, on the basis of its use as residential-agricultural property, when the contiguous land surrounding it had been rezoned to commercial-apartment use.


OVERVIEW: At issue was the propriety of application of restrictions to property as a limitation on evidence as to value. Montgomery Co., Md. Code § 111-48f froze the value of land to be acquired for a public purpose. The court held that the property owner had to be permitted to defend himself against that ordinance. The ordinance directly impinged upon one of his constitutional guarantees. To hold otherwise was to deprive him of the opportunity to receive just compensation for his property guaranteed by Md. Const. art. III, § 40. The court held that Montgomery Co., Md. Code § 111-48f was unconstitutional and of no legal effect. Accordingly, it was error for the lower court to admit into evidence testimony as to the market value of the property based on R-A zoning as the highest and best use of the property. The court did not think that evidence as to the market value of the condemned property, based on the R-A zoning classification frozen by ordinance, should have been admitted over the objections of the property owner. A verdict which could have been predicated on such evidence of value would constitute a taking of the property without just compensation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case for a new trial.

 

598 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Stovall v. Secretary of State


No. 85, September Term, 1968


Court of Appeals of Maryland


252 Md. 258; 250 A.2d 107; 1969 Md. LEXIS 1082



February 4, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County; Evans, J.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed. Costs to be paid by the appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a decision of the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland), which sustained, without leave to amend, appellee government officials' demurrer to their petition for an order declaring 1963 Md. Laws 41, § 1 to be null and void and for broad injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to 1963 Md. Laws 41, § 1 Morgan State College, which had been controlled by its own board of Trustees, was placed under the structure of the State College system. The taxpayers, one of whom was an alumnus of Morgan State College, alleged that the statute was vague and indefinite, and the contingencies which would allow the State College system to take control had not occurred. The trial court concluded they were without standing to sue and that they were barred by laches. On appeal, the court affirmed. There was no dispute about their failure to allege an illegal expenditure of tax-derived funds resulting in either a pecuniary loss to them or an increase in their taxes. Their contention that all persons who had degrees from Morgan State College acquired significant legal status, rights, recognition, and benefits which the failed to restore was completely baseless. While they argued on appeal that the transfer of control resulted in increased taxes, their pleadings neither supported that conclusion nor necessarily implied that pecuniary loss or increased taxes had been alleged.


OUTCOME: The judgment dismissing the taxpayers' action challenging the transfer of control of Morgan State College to the State College system was affirmed.

 

599 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


State v. Sedacca


No. 9, September Term, 1968


Court of Appeals of Maryland


252 Md. 207; 249 A.2d 456; 1969 Md. LEXIS 1075



January 21, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals of Maryland.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Special Appeals reversed and case remanded to the Circuit Court for Harford County for distribution to the proper officials of Harford County of the $ 500 fine presently held in a special account in accordance with the order of June 7, 1966 of the Circuit Court for Harford County, the appellee, Morris Sedacca, to pay the costs in this Court, in the Court of Special Appeals and in the Circuit Court for Harford County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order from the Court of Special Appeals of Maryland, which found that a search and seizure was unlawful and that it was unnecessary to consider two constitutional issues raised by appellee in regard to Md. Ann. Code art. 81, § 455 (1957), following defendant's conviction for transportation and possession of untaxed cigarettes in violation of § 455.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was convicted in a trial court of transportation and possession of untaxed cigarettes in violation of Md. Ann. Code art. 81, § 455 (1957). A lower court found that appellee's search and seizure was unlawful, and that it was unnecessary to consider two constitutional issued raised by appellee in regard to § 455. The state sought review of the lower court's judgment by writ of certiorari. The court reversed the lower court's judgment and remanded the case. The court determined that the provisions of § 455 were not invalid as unreasonable restraints upon interstate commerce or as regulations in an interstate commerce regulatory area in which Congress had preempted the field. The court found that a person of common intelligence construing § 455 would have been adequately informed that if he was transporting unstamped cigarettes through Maryland, that he had to have the required documents or was guilty of the misdemeanor provided in the statute. The court found further that a search pursuant to a lawful arrest was perfectly legal. In the court's opinion, the search and seizure in appellee's case was not unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded a lower court's judgment, which found that appellee's search and seizure was unlawful and that it was unnecessary to consider two constitutional issues regarding a Maryland statute because the provisions of the statute were not invalid as unreasonable restraints upon interstate commerce or as regulations in which Congress had preempted the field, and the search and seizure in appellee's case was not unreasonable.

 

600 of 1543 DOCUMENTS


Minor v. Shifflett


No. 7, September Term, 1968


Court of Appeals of Maryland


252 Md. 158; 249 A.2d 159; 1969 Md. LEXIS 1072



January 15, 1969, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Motion for Rehearing Filed February 14, 1969; Denied March 3, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: Two appeals in one record from the Circuit Court for Baltimore County; Maguire, J.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, costs to be paid by the appellees.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of the judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland), which affirmed the decision of a county board of zoning appeals, which reclassified a five-acre parcel of appellee landowner's property, on which the landowner operated an automobile junkyard.


OVERVIEW: The landowners operated an automobile junkyard on their property located in a residential zone. Their use was nonconforming since the adoption of zoning in the county. The landowner applied to the county's zoning commissioner for a reclassification of his property and for the granting of a special exception pursuant to the county's zoning law and zoning regulations. The request was denied. On appeal, reclassification was granted to a five-acre parcel of the land, but denied on the remainder. That decision was affirmed by the lower court. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the landowner failed to meet his burden of proving that the zoning classifications were invalid. The court found that the presence of a nonconforming use in that area was not evidence of error in original zoning requiring a change of classification. The court held that no evidence was presented that the use contemplated for the landowner's property was the only use for which it was fitted, and that there was no evidence of any substantial change in the character of the neighborhood.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court.
